<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The chromosome-scale assembly of the willow genome provides insight into Salicaceae genome evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-04-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Salix suchowensis is an early-flowering shrub willow that provides a desirable system for studies on the basic biology of woody plants. The current reference genome of S. suchowensis was assembled with 454 sequencing reads. Here, we report a chromosome-scale assembly of S. suchowensis generated by combining PacBio sequencing with Hi-C technologies. The obtained genome assemblies covered a total length of 356&#8201;Mb. The contig N50 of these assemblies was 263,908&#8201;bp, which was ~65-fold higher than that reported previously. The contiguity and completeness of the genome were significantly improved. By applying Hi-C data, 339.67&#8201;Mb (95.29%) of the assembled sequences were allocated to the 19 chromosomes of haploid willow. With the chromosome-scale assembly, we revealed a series of major chromosomal fissions and fusions that explain the genome divergence between the sister genera of Salix and Populus. The more complete and accurate willow reference genome obtained in this study provides a fundamental resource for studying many genetic and genomic characteristics of woody plants."/>

                                    </a>
                                </li>
                            
                        </ul>
                    </div>
                </div>
            </div>

                    <ul class="c-author-list js-etal-collapsed" data-etal="25" data-etal-small="3" data-test="authors-list" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Suyun-Wei" data-author-popup="auth-Suyun-Wei">Suyun Wei</a></span><span class="u-js-hide">Â 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-3262-2771"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-3262-2771</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Nanjing Forestry University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.410625.4, Key Laboratory for Tree Breeding and Germplasm Improvement, Southern Modern Forestry Collaborative Innovation Center, College of Forestry, Nanjing Forestry University, Nanjing, 210037, China" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Nanjing Forestry University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.410625.4, College of Information Science and Technology, Nanjing Forestry University, Nanjing, 210037, China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Yonghua-Yang" data-author-popup="auth-Yonghua-Yang">Yonghua Yang</a></span><span class="u-js-hide">Â

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Salix suchowensis</i> is an early-flowering shrub willow that provides a desirable system for studies on the basic biology of woody plants. The current reference genome of <i>S. suchowensis</i> was assembled with 454 sequencing reads. Here, we report a chromosome-scale assembly of <i>S. suchowensis</i> generated by combining PacBio sequencing with Hi-C technologies. The obtained genome assemblies covered a total length of 356âMb. The contig N50 of these assemblies was 263,908âbp, which was ~65-fold higher than that reported previously. The contiguity and completeness of the genome were significantly improved. By applying Hi-C data, 339.67âMb (95.29%) of the assembled sequences were allocated to the 19 chromosomes of haploid willow. With the chromosome-scale assembly, we revealed a series of major chromosomal fissions and fusions that explain the genome divergence between the sister genera of <i>Salix</i> and <i>Populus</i>. The more complete and accurate willow reference genome obtained in this study provides a fundamental resource for studying many genetic and genomic characteristics of woody plants.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

            <span>Download PDF</span>
            <svg aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xlink:href="#global-icon-download"/></svg>
        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The Salicaceae family contains the sister genera of <i>Salix</i> (willows) and <i>Populus</i> (poplars), which are diecious catkin-bearing woody plants. Willows and poplars are distributed widely in many regions of the world. They exhibit high wood production and are commercially important in artificial plantations. Within the Salicaceae family, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> was the first sequenced woody plant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d19879e410">1</a></sup>, making it a widely adopted model system for various types of genetic studies on tree species. The <i>P. trichocarpa</i> v3.0 assembly is ~430âMb, with a contig N50 and scaffold N50 of 205âkb and 8âMb, respectively. A total of 41,335 protein-coding genes are predicted in the <i>P. trichocarpa</i> genome. In recent years, the genomes of <i>P. euphratica</i> Oliv<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Ma, T. et al. Genomic insights into salt adaptation in a desert poplar. Nat. Commun. 4, 2797 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d19879e424">2</a></sup>, <i>P. pruinosa</i> Schrenk<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Yang, W. et al. The draft genome sequence of a desert tree Populus pruinosa. GigaScience 6, gix075 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d19879e431">3</a></sup>, <i>P. alba</i> var. <i>pyramidalis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Ma, J. et al. Genome sequence and genetic transformation of a widely distributed and cultivated poplar. Plant Biotechnol. J. 17, 451â460 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d19879e441">4</a></sup>, <i>P. alba</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Liu, Y., Wang, X. &amp; Zeng, Q. De novo assembly of white poplar genome and genetic diversity of white poplar population in Irtysh River basin in China. Sci. China Life Sci. 62, 609â618 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d19879e447">5</a></sup>, <i>P. tremula</i>, and <i>P. tremuloides</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Lin, Y. et al. Functional and evolutionary genomic inferences in Populus through genome and population sequencing of American and European aspen. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. 115, E10970âE10978 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d19879e456">6</a></sup> have also been sequenced, assembled, and annotated. Moreover, the first draft genome assemblies of a <i>P. deltoides</i> (<a href="http://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov">http://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov</a>) and a <i>P. tremula</i>Ã<i>alba</i> hybrid (<a href="http://aspendb.uga.edu/s717">http://aspendb.uga.edu/s717</a>) have been made publicly available online. Compared to poplars, whole-genome-sequencing studies on willow species have been lagging behind; only the genome sequences of <i>S. purpurea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Nordberg, H. et al. The genome portal of the Department of Energy Joint Genome Institute: 2014 updates. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D26âD31 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d19879e481">7</a></sup> and <i>S. suchowensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d19879e487">8</a></sup> have been reported. The genome of <i>S. purpurea</i> (<a href="http://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov">http://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov</a>) was assembled with Illumina sequencing reads, resulting in a genome assembly of 392âMb. Within this assembly, 276âMb (~70%) of sequences were anchored along different willow chromosomes aided by a genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Nordberg, H. et al. The genome portal of the Department of Energy Joint Genome Institute: 2014 updates. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D26âD31 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d19879e498">7</a></sup>. For <i>S. suchowensis</i>, a genome assembly covering a total length of 303.8âMb was obtained from Roche-454 sequencing reads. Anchored with a genetic map constructed for the sequenced individual, 229.2âMb of sequence assemblies were positioned onto the 19 chromosomes of the <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d19879e509">8</a></sup>.</p><p>Salicaceae species possess relatively small genomes. However, it is difficult to achieve high-quality genome assemblies for these species using short-read sequencing technologies due to the high heterozygosity and abundant repetitive sequences in their genomes. There is an active demand to improve the currently assembled <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome, which shows inaccuracy and low contiguity. In recent years, the single-molecule, real-time (SMRT) PacBio<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Eid, J. et al. Real-time DNA sequencing from single polymerase molecules. Science 323, 133â138 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d19879e519">9</a></sup> sequencing and chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Dekker, J. The threeâCâs of chromosome conformation capture: controls, controls, controls. Nat. Methods 3, 17 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d19879e523">10</a></sup> techniques have been used to make significant advances in improving the assembly of plant genomes at the chromosomal level. Single-molecule sequencing can generate reads with an average length of &gt;10âkb, which overcomes the restriction of the short reads generated from the Illumina sequencing platform. Additionally, Hi-C technology captures chromatin interactions within the nucleus, which can be used to infer the location of unanchored contigs and, thus, help to reconstruct haplotypes at the chromosomal level. Recently, several high-quality plant genomes have been obtained by using PacBio sequencing and Hi-C technologies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, L. et al. Improved Brassica rapa reference genome by single-molecule sequencing and chromosome conformation capture technologies. Horticulture Res. 5, 50 (2018)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d19879e527">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524 (2017)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d19879e527_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Jibran, R. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of the black raspberry (Rubus occidentalis L.) genome based on chromatin interaction data. Horticulture Res. 5, 8 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d19879e530">13</a></sup>.</p><p>According to the taxonomy of Salicaceae, there are only ~29 species in genus <i>Populus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Echenwalder, J. (eds). Systematics and Evolution of Populus. Biology of Populus Ands It Implications for Management and Conservation. Part I. (NRC Research Press, 1996)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d19879e539">14</a></sup>. By contrast, over 300 species have been distinguished in genus <i>Salix</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Argus, G. W. Infrageneric classification of Salix (Salicaceae) in the new world. Syst. Bot. Monogr. 52, 1â121 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d19879e545">15</a></sup>. Poplars generally take the form of large trees, while willows exhibit different growth forms, including large trees, subtrees and small shrubs. Poplars and willows are not only important industrial fiber resources but are also widely used for landscaping. <i>S. suchowensis</i> is a small shrub that normally arrives at sexual maturity at one year of age. This species also possesses other characteristics that are favorable for basic genetic studies, such as ease of vegetative propagation and a predisposition to hybridization. Taken together, these characteristics make <i>S. suchowensis</i> an ideal model system for revealing the genetic mechanisms underlying the unique biological processes of woody plants. In this study, we aimed to obtain a highly contiguous, chromosome-scale genome assembly by combining PacBio sequencing and Hi-C technologies. The new assembly provides a much better reference genome for <i>S. suchowensis</i>, which will facilitate studies on Salicaceae species and serve as a valuable genomic resource for the woody plant research community.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>In this study, we generated a total of 33.29âGb PacBio sequencing reads from the SMRT sequencing platform, achieving ~87x coverage of the <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome (Suupplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S1</a>). First, the raw PacBio sequencing reads were self-corrected and assembled, which produced assembly groups containing 5346 contigs. Then, 902 redundant contigs (alternative contigs) were identified and removed through sequence alignment analysis. Finally, 3444 primary contigs were retained to reconstruct the haploid pseudomolecule for each chromosome. The obtained genome assembly contains 3444 contigs, covering a total length of 356.5âMb, with a GC content of 34.94% and a contig N50 of 263.9âKb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S2</a>). The 373 largest contigs cover &gt;50% of the genome length, and 1926 large contigs represent ~90% of the genome.</p><p>In total, 40.48âGb clean reads were obtained from Hi-C sequencing, covering over 98x of the <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S3</a>). We assessed the quality of the Hi-C data, in which 92.56% of Hi-C sequencing reads were mapped to the assembled contigs, 53.60% of which were unique mapped read pairs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S3</a>). Integrating 81.47% of the valid interaction pairs from the unique paired alignments, the PacBio sequencing assemblies were categorized and ordered to construct chromosome-scale scaffolds, resulting in a total of 1201 contigs, with a scaffold N50 of 16.8âMb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Tab1">1</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Fig1">1</a>). Then, the derived contigs were assembled into 19 pseudomolecules by means of agglomerative hierarchical clustering and the use of LACHESIS analysis tools. In total, 339.7âMb of sequences were anchored along the 19 chromosomes in willow, representing 95.28% of the assembled contigs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S4</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Fig2">2</a>). Among the anchored contigs (2913), 2262 contigs (309.88âMb) were oriented on the 19 pseudochromosomes. The longest and shortest pseudomolecules were chromosomes I and XII, with lengths of 34.97âMb and 10.67âMb, respectively.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics for <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v1.0 and srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0268-6/MediaObjects/41438_2020_268_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Hi-C contact data mapped on the updated <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0 showing genome-wide all-by-all interactions</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation</h3><p>We identified a total of 168.5âMb of non-redundant repetitive elements (47.27% of the genome assembly), 28.27% of which were retrotransposons and 5.32% of which were DNA transposons (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S5</a>), which was higher than the percentage in the previous assembly (125.6âMb, 41.4%). Similar to <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v1.0, long terminal retrotransposons (LTRs) were found to dominate (21.84%) the repetitive sequences. Among these sequences, <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> LTRs represented 10.86% and 10.53% of the total genome length, respectively, and accounted for 24 and 23% of the identified repetitive elements, (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Fig1">1</a>), whereas non-LTR elements spanned a physical length of 6.67âMb and accounted for 1.87% of the genome length. In <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0, the class II elements (DNA transposons) represented 5.32% of the genome length. Further analysis revealed that the abundance of repetitive elements in the assembled chromosomes (1432/Mb) was much higher than that in the unanchored scaffolds (378/Mb). By contrast, the content of repetitive sequences was much higher in unanchored scaffolds than in the assembled chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d19879e796">8</a></sup>. This comparison indicates that the negative effect of repetitive sequences on genome assembly can be effectively overwhelmed by long sequence reads.</p><p>Sequence annotation predicted 36,937 protein-coding genes in <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0 by combining the de novo, homology-based and transcriptome-based predictions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM1">S1</a>). The average gene size was 3440âbp, with 5.6 exons per gene (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Tab1">1</a>). Among the predicted genes, 31,863 were anchored on the 19 chromosomes and covered a total length of 115.10âMb, with a total exon length of 40.68âMb (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S6</a>). Based on high centromere-associated repeat abundance and a low gene content relative to the other regions of the chromosomes, the centromeres was predicted and positioned in each chromosome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Fig1">1</a>), which revealed four acrocentric chromosomes (Chr_III, Chr_IX, Chr XIV, Chr_XVIII) and 15 submetacentric chromosomes in the genome of <i>S. suchowensis</i>. Among the predicted protein-coding genes, 30,260 (81.92%) were supported by transcriptome data from at least one of five tissues, including tender roots, young leaves, vegetative buds, nonlignified stems and bark (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S7</a>). In total, 36,150 (97.87%) predicted genes showed functional annotations in at least one protein database (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S8</a>). Conserved domain features were detected in 40.6% of the protein-coding genes. Additionally, we further annotated the noncoding RNAs and predicted 846 tranfer RNA (tRNA) genes, 261 ribosomal RNA (rRNA) genes, and 172 microRNA (miRNA) genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S9</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genomic comparison</h3><p>In this study, we evaluated the genome assemblies of <i>S. suchowensis</i> v1.0 and <i>S. suchowensis</i> v2.0 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S10</a>). We sequenced seven randomly selected bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs) of <i>S. suchowensis</i> genomic DNA. Sequence alignment showed that <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0 covered 99.2% of the BAC sequences, while <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v1.0 covered 93.1% of the BAC sequences (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S11</a>). The BAC sequence alignment also showed that v2.0 exhibited better continuity and higher precision than v1.0. We further evaluated the quality of the new assembly with ~30âGb of Illumina paired-end reads. The mapping ratio for the Illumina reads was 78.11% for v1.0, which increased to 90.38% for v2.0 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S12</a>). To assess the gene coverage, a total of 85,962 unigenes derived from RNA-Seq data for <i>S. suchowensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d19879e869">8</a></sup> were mapped to the two versions of the genome assemblies. More than 99.4% of these unigenes could be mapped to v2.0 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S13</a>), while ~97.6% of them could be mapped to v1.0. The completeness of the predicted genes in v2.0 was also assessed by sequence alignment with 1440 conserved Embryophyta genes from Benchmarking Universal Single-copy Orthologs (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d19879e877">16</a></sup>, and the results showed that coverage of these genes was 94.8% and 89.6% for v2.0 and v1.0, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S10</a>).</p><p>A total of 30,265 genes in <i>S. suchowensis</i> v2.0 were divided into 21,107 gene clusters, among which 18,692 gene clusters (23,397 genes) were shared with <i>S. suchowensis</i> v1.0, and 20,086 gene clusters (28,127 genes) were shared with <i>P. trichocarpa</i> v3.0 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM1">S2</a>). The comparison of the protein-coding genes in <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v1.0 with those in <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0, revealed that 25,891 genes in v1.0 were highly homologous to 31,694 genes in v2.0, indicating that the annotated genes in the updated genome were more complete than those in the previous version and included more multicopy genes. Furthermore, 5243 version-specific genes in v2.0 were analyzed for their distribution on the 19 chromosomes and among the unclustered contigs. Among these genes, 1743 were distributed among unclustered contigs, and the rest were distributed on the assembled chromosomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S14</a>). GO enrichment analysis showed that the functions of these version-specific genes mainly included the respiratory electron transport chain, NADH dehydrogenase activity, ATP synthesis-coupled electron transport, and oxidative binding (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM1">S3</a>). The better gene coverage of the updated genome will greatly facilitate functional genomics studies in the future.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene family analysis</h3><p>We characterized some important gene families in <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0. <i>R</i> genes containing nucleotide-binding site (NBS) domains play a vital role in conferring resistance to plant disease. In <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, 402<i>R</i> genes containing an NBS domain were detected<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Kohler, A. et al. Genome-wide identification of NBS resistance genes in Populus trichocarpa. Plant Mol. Biol. 66, 619â636 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d19879e932">17</a></sup>, including 143 <i>coiled-coil-NBS-LRR</i> (<i>CNL</i>) genes, 93 <i>TIR-NBS-LRR</i> (<i>TNL</i>) genes, 115 <i>NBS-LRR</i> (<i>NL</i>) genes, and 51 <i>NBS</i> genes, whereas 346<i>R</i> genes containing an NBS domain were identified in <i>S. suchowensis</i> v2.0, including 132 <i>CNL</i> genes, 83 <i>TNL</i> genes, 123 <i>NL</i> genes, and eight <i>NBS</i> genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Fig3">3</a>), suggesting either faster shrinkage or slower gain of <i>R</i> genes containing an NBS domain in <i>S. suchowensis</i> than in <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, especially for the <i>NBS</i> genes (51 vs. 8). In <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, chromosome XIX contains &gt;70<i>R</i> genes, most of which are clustered at the peritelomeric end of this chromosome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bresson, A. et al. Qualitative and quantitative resistances to leaf rust finely mapped within two nucleotide-binding site leucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR)-rich genomic regions of chromosome 19 in poplar. N. Phytologist 192, 151â163 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1000">18</a></sup>. In <i>S. suchowensis</i> v2.0, chromosome XIX contains 107<i>R</i> genes, most of which are also located at the peritelomeric end.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Phylogenetic tree of the nucleotide-binding site (NBS) domain R gene family in S. suchowensis genome v2.0."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Phylogenetic tree of the nucleotide-binding site (NBS) domain <i>R</i> gene family in <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0268-6/MediaObjects/41438_2020_268_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0268-6/MediaObjects/41438_2020_268_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="638" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Each protein is encoded with the gene number and domains present. Different colors correspond to different subfamilies. Distinct expression profiles of gene members based on RNA-Seq revealed different expression patterns among five tissues, including tender roots, young leaves, vegetative buds, nonlignified stems, and bark<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1024">8</a></sup></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Wood formation is a unique biological process that occurs in woody plants. In this study, we dedicated special efforts to the analysis of genes involved in wood formation. Wood mainly consists of cellulose, hemicellulose and lignin. We annotated 28 gene families involved in cell wall formation, which included 296 genes involved in cellulose and hemicellulose biosynthesis, including ten fucosyltransferase (FUT) genes, six xyloglucan xylosyltransferase (XXT) genes, five hydroxyproline-rich glycoprotease (RGP) genes, 275 glycoside hydrolase (GH) genes, and 75 genes involved in lignin biosynthesis in <i>S. suchowensis</i> v2.0 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S15</a>). Phylogenetic analysis showed that the numbers of FUT, XXT, and RGP orthologous genes are similar in <i>S. suchowensis</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Fig4">4</a>), but the number of GH orthologous genes in <i>Arabidopsis</i> is significantly lower than that in Salicaceae (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM1">S4</a>), especially for the GH17, GH18, and GH31 subfamilies. We propose that these gene subfamilies might play a role in secondary cell wall formation, which is a process unique to woody plants. Lignin is an amorphous polymer related to cellulose that provides rigidity and, together with cellulose, forms the woody cell walls of plants. In <i>S. suchowensis</i> v2.0, the 75 genes involved in lignin biosynthesis were clustered in ten gene families (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM1">S5</a>). In general, willow contains more genes involved in lignin biosynthesis than <i>Arabidopsis</i> (75 vs. 34) and fewer genes involved in lignin biosynthesis than <i>Populus</i> (75 vs. 90). In detail, the genes coding p-coumarate-3-hydroxylase (C3H), trans-cinnamate 4-hydroxylase (C4H), caffeic acid O-methyltransferase (COMT), ferulate-5-hydroxylase (F5H), and hydroxycinnamoyl-CoA shikimate/quinate hydroxycinnamoyl transferase (HCT) occur in multiple copies in <i>S. suchowensis</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, whereas they occur in a single-copy in <i>A. thaliana</i>. In particular, COMT, a potential target enzyme for modifying the composition of lignin in plants, is a single-copy gene in <i>A. thaliana</i>, while there are nine and 13 copies of <i>COMT</i> in <i>S. suchowensis</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S15</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Phylogenetic analysis of genes involved in cellulose and hemicellulose biosynthesis in Salix, Populus, and Arabidopsis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Phylogenetic analysis of genes involved in cellulose and hemicellulose biosynthesis in <i>Salix</i>, <i>Populus</i>, and <i>Arabidopsis</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0268-6/MediaObjects/41438_2020_268_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0268-6/MediaObjects/41438_2020_268_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="685" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Gene names starting with âPotriâ are genes of <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, âEVMâ indicates genes of <i>S. suchowensis</i>, and those starting with âATâ are genes of <i>A. thaliana</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Willows are characterized by an unusually high content of salicylic acid in their secondary metabolites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Santner, A. &amp; Estelle, M. Recent advances and emerging trends in plant hormone signalling. Nature 459, 1071 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1125">19</a></sup>. Salicylic acid is a phytohormone that regulates signal transduction pathways involved in the defense against biotic and abiotic stresses. Gene annotation showed that there were 827 genes involved in salicylic acid biosynthesis and metabolism in <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0. The KEGG pathway analysis indicated that these genes were enriched in plant immune signaling to induce systemic acquired resistance, such as plant hormone signal transduction, MAPK signaling pathway-plant, plant-pathogen interaction, and alpha-linolenic acid metabolism pathways (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM1">S6</a>). The number of genes related to salicylic acid biosynthesis and metabolism is much higher in <i>Populus</i> than in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (1397 vs. 125, respectively), suggesting that salicylic acid may be involved in signal transduction processes specific to trees, such as plant hormone signal transduction and wood formation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Whole-genome duplication analysis</h3><p>We detected 11,037 and 15,092 paralogous gene pairs within the genomes of <i>S. suchowensis</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, respectively. We also identified 12,710 single-copy orthologous gene pairs between these two lineages. We calculated the fourfold synonymous third-codon transversion (4DTV) value for each gene pair. Plotting the 4DTV values for the paralogous gene pairs revealed two peaks in both willow and poplar (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Fig5">5a, b</a>). The peak associated with salicoid duplication appeared at 4DTV values of 0.101 and 0.082âinâ<i>S. suchowensis</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, respectively. Plotting the 4DTV values for single-copy orthologous gene pairs revealed a peak at a 4DTV value of 0.04 that was associated with the divergence of these two lineages (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Fig5">5c</a>). The comparison of the peak positions showed that the divergence of poplar and willow occurred after salicoid duplication in each lineage. The conclusion was further confirmed by analyzing the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (Ks) for each gene pair. Salicoid duplication occurred at Ks values of 0.365 inâ<i>S. suchowensis</i> and 0.283 inâ<i>P. trichocarpa</i>, and the divergence of these two lineages occurred at a Ks value of 0.158 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Fig5">5dâf</a>). Notably, the peaks associated with salicoid duplication were found to not overlap in these two lineages. The same scenario was also observed in previous reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hou, J., Wei, S., Pan, H., Zhuge, Q. &amp; Yin, T. Uneven selection pressure accelerating divergence of Populus and Salix. Horticulture Res. 6, 37 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1178">20</a></sup>. The analysis of nucleotide substitution rates revealed that this characteristic was caused by the molecular clock clicking more slowly in willow than in poplar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1182">8</a></sup>. The visualization of synteny blocks (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Fig6">6</a>) revealed high collinearity in <i>S. suchowensis</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> for 17 chromosomes. As found in previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hou, J. et al. Major chromosomal rearrangements distinguish willow and poplar after the ancestral âSalicoidâ genome duplication. Genome Biol. Evol. 8, 1868â1875 (2016)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1196">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hanley, S., Mallott, M. &amp; Karp, A. Alignment of a Salix linkage map to the Populus genomic sequence reveals macrosynteny between willow and poplar genomes. Tree Genet. Genomes 3, 35â48 (2006)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1196_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Berlin, S., Lagercrantz, U., von Arnold, S., Ãst, T. &amp; RÃ¶nnberg-WÃ¤stljung, A. C. High-density linkage mapping and evolution of paralogs and orthologs in Salix and Populus. BMC Genomics 11, 129 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1199">23</a></sup>, large interchromosomal rearrangements were detected between chromosome I and chromosome XVI, suggesting that major chromosomal fusions and fissions occurred after salicoid duplication, leading to the divergence of the two sister genera.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Four-fold synonymous third-codon transversion (4DTV) and the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (Ks) for each homologous gene pair in S. suchowensis genome v2.0 and P. trichocarpa genome v3.0."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Four-fold synonymous third-codon transversion (4DTV) and the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (Ks) for each homologous gene pair in <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0 and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> genome v3.0.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure"
