<i>S. suchowensis</i> v2.0, <b>e</b> Ks values for paralogs in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> v3.0, <b>f</b> Ks values for single-copy orthologs between <i>S. suchowensis</i> v2.0 and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> v3.0</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0268-6/MediaObjects/41438_2020_268_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0268-6/MediaObjects/41438_2020_268_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="180" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Syntenic comparison of the homologous chromosomes between <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0 and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> genome v3.0</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Furthermore, we examined the extent of sequence divergence by estimating the number of nonsynonymous substitutions per nonsynonymous site (Ka)/Ks for paralogous genes arising from salicoid duplication in both <i>S. suchowensis</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Fig7">7</a>). The average Ka/Ks value was 0.286 for <i>S. suchowensis</i> and 0.311 for <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, suggesting that salicoid duplicated genes are subject to stronger purifying selection in <i>S. suchowensis</i> than in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S16</a>). At the chromosome level, the average Ka/Ks values for the salicoid duplicated genes ranged from 0.30 to 0.34 for the chromosomes of <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, whereas the corresponding values ranged from 0.27 to 0.3âinâ<i>S. suchowensis</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#MOESM2">S16</a>). Thus, stronger purifying selection was observed for each chromosome in <i>S. suchowensis</i> than in <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, which would lead to the faster loss of duplicated genes in willow than in poplar and ultimately result in a smaller gene content in <i>S. suchowensis</i> than in <i>P. trichocarpa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hou, J., Wei, S., Pan, H., Zhuge, Q. &amp; Yin, T. Uneven selection pressure accelerating divergence of Populus and Salix. Horticulture Res. 6, 37 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1325">20</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Fig. 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0268-6/MediaObjects/41438_2020_268_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0268-6/MediaObjects/41438_2020_268_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="204" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>Boxplot of Ka/Ks values for the paralogous genes that arose from salicoid duplication on each chromosome in <i>S. suchowensis</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>We proposed an improved chromosome-scale assembly of the <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome using PacBio long reads and Hi-C technology. The emergence of PacBio sequencing has led to a significant increase in contig N50 sizes compared to previous short-read assemblies. The first assembly of the <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome based on Sanger sequencing presents limitations due to its fragmentation and low continuity (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#Tab1">1</a>). By combining single-molecule sequencing and Hi-C technology, <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0 spanning 356.5âMb was obtained, and the contig and scaffold N50s are 264âKb and 16.8âMb, respectively, indicating high contiguity of the assembly. Despite the high heterozygosity of the <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome, we used Hi-C technology to accurately assign the final assembled contigs to the chromosomal positions and constructed 19 chromosomes of the <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome, accounting for 95.29% of the assembly. We accurately compared the quality of <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0 with that of the previous short-read assembly from a variety of aspects, verifying that our updated assembly achieved higher contiguity and quality in its extensive coverage of the genome and higher mapping ratios of the genomic reads, RNA-Seq short reads and conserved genes, suggesting that our assembly is a good alternative resource for diploid willow.</p><p>Compared with poplar genomes, willow genomes are generally smaller and contain fewer protein-coding genes. Using k-mer analysis, it was estimated that the genome size of <i>S. suchowensis</i> was ~425âMb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1379">8</a></sup>, while that of <i>S. purpurea</i> was ~450âMb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Nordberg, H. et al. The genome portal of the Department of Energy Joint Genome Institute: 2014 updates. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D26âD31 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1386">7</a></sup>, both of which are slightly smaller than the estimated 485âMbâ<i>P. trichocarpa</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1394">1</a></sup>. The assembled genome size of <i>S. purpurea</i> v1.0 is ~392âMb, with 37,865 putative protein-coding genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Nordberg, H. et al. The genome portal of the Department of Energy Joint Genome Institute: 2014 updates. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D26âD31 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1401">7</a></sup>, while the sizes of the recently released <i>S. purpurea</i> genome v5.1 and v3.1 are ~329âMb and ~313âMb, with predicted coding genes of 35,125 and 34,464, respectively. The published genome size of <i>S. suchowensis</i> v1.0 is ~304âMb, with only 24,931 putative protein-coding genes on 19 chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Hou, J. et al. Different autosomes evolved into sex chromosomes in the sister genera of Salix and Populus. Sci. Rep. 5, 9076 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1411">24</a></sup>. Using PacBio sequencing long reads, the size of our improved genome assembly for <i>S. suchowensis</i> was 356.5âMb, and 36,937 putative protein-encoding genes were predicted in the whole genome. With the obvious improvement in the contiguity of <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0, a greater number of genes were annotated than in the previous assembly. However, only 31,863 anchored and oriented genes were found on 19 chromosomes of the <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome, and the average Ka/Ks ratios of salicoid duplicated gene pairs on each chromosome in <i>S. suchowensis</i> were smaller than those in <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, suggesting that willow might be subjected to stronger purifying selection, which is consistent with previous studies indicating that willows have evolved faster than poplars, resulting in a reduced number of predicted genes and an overall smaller genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1431">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hou, J., Wei, S., Pan, H., Zhuge, Q. &amp; Yin, T. Uneven selection pressure accelerating divergence of Populus and Salix. Horticulture Res. 6, 37 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1434">20</a></sup>.</p><p>In summary, the development of genomic assembly technologies makes it possible to produce chromosome-level scaffolds in a rapid and cost-effective way. The updated <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0 includes a high-quality chromosomal-level assembly and many important genes, offering novel insights into genome evolution, functional innovation and key regulatory pathways in <i>Salix</i> and providing excellent genetic resources for comparative genome studies among Salicaceae species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome sequencing</h3><p>The same material of female cultivated shrub willow used for the first assembly was selected from the germplasm resources of <i>S. suchowensis</i> preserved at Nanjing Forestry University. Fresh young leaves were collected and immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen for genomic DNA isolation and library construction. According to the recommendations of Pacific Biosciences, genomic DNA was fragmented to a target size of ~20âkb to construct the PacBio genome sequencing library. All the DNA libraries were sequenced using the PacBio platform (Pacific Biosciences, CA, USA) with P6-C4 sequencing chemistry. The genomic DNA was sheared to construct short insertion paired-end (PE) libraries following the manufacturerâs instructions (Illumina, CA, USA). All libraries were sequenced at 2Ã100âbp on the Illumina HiSeqâXâten platform. All sequencing procedures were performed by the BioMarker Technologies Company (Beijing, China).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Genome assembly</h3><p>The PacBio SMRT-Analysis package (<a href="https://www.pacb.com">https://www.pacb.com</a>) was used for the quality control of the raw polymerase reads; sequencing adaptors were removed, and low-quality short reads were filtered. Owing to the high error rate of the raw PacBio long reads, we first used the error correction module embedded in Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1474">25</a></sup> to correct these errors. Then, the high-quality PacBio clean reads were de novo assembled by using Canu with default parameters, which produced assembly agglomerations containing 5346 contigs for the preliminary assembly. Owing to the high heterozygosity of the <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome, the clean PacBio subreads were aligned back to the assembled contigs by using BLASR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chaisson, M. J. &amp; Tesler, G. Mapping single molecule sequencing reads using basic local alignment with successive refinement (BLASR): application and theory. BMC Bioinforma. 13, 238 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1481">26</a></sup>, and the contigs were further corrected with Quiver from the SMRT-Analysis package. The Illumina short reads obtained from the <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome were aligned onto the optimized contigs using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, H. Toward better understanding of artifacts in variant calling from high-coverage samples. Bioinformatics 30, 2843â2851 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1489">27</a></sup>, and parsing was performed with SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1493">28</a></sup>. As a result, 3444 primary contigs were retained to reconstruct the haploid pseudomolecule for each chromosome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Chromosome-scale assembly with Hi-C data</h3><p>Hi-C libraries were constructed from fresh leaf tissue of <i>S. suchowensis</i>, which is the same line that we sequenced for genome assembly. The Hi-C libraries were sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq X ten platform (Illumina, CA, USA) at the BioMarker Technologies Company (Beijing, China). Owing to the limitation of the short reads involved in the Hi-C procedure, we used HiC-Pro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Servant, N. et al. HiC-Pro: an optimized and flexible pipeline for Hi-C data processing. Genome Biol. 16, 259 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1508">29</a></sup> to process these Hi-C sequencing data, including the mapping of the Hi-C sequencing reads to the assembled contigs with the BWA-aln aligning algorithm without any mismatches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with BurrowsâWheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1512">30</a></sup> and with the detection of valid contacts. The preassembled contigs split into segments of 50âkb on average, combined with uniquely matched Hi-C data, were clustered, ordered and directed onto the pseudochromosomes with LACHESIS software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1516">31</a></sup>. To improve the chromosome-scale assembly quality, manual adjustment of orientation errors with obvious discrete chromatin interaction patterns was performed. The final chromosome assemblies were divided into 100âkb bins with equal lengths, and the interaction signals generated by the valid mapped read pairs between each bin were visualized in a heat map.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Genome annotation</h3><p>We used a combination of de novo prediction and homology-based searches to annotate transposable elements in the genome. To construct a de novo repeat library, the specific transposable elements in our assembly were first screened using LTR FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1528">32</a></sup>, RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351âi358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1532">33</a></sup> and PILER-DF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Edgar, R. C. &amp; Myers, E. W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21, i152âi158 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1536">34</a></sup>, and these repetitive sequences were then classified into families with PASTEClassifier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1540">35</a></sup>. Finally, RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinforma. 25, 4.10.11â14.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1544">36</a></sup> was applied to perform a homology-based repeat search using both the de novo repeat library and the known Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenetic Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1549">37</a></sup> transposable elements database.</p><p>The repeat-masked genome assembly was used for annotating gene models through a combination of ab initio prediction, homology-based prediction and RNA-Seq-assisted prediction methods. Five different ab initio gene prediction programs were used to identify gene models. In the homology-based prediction, protein sequences from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, and <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i> downloaded from Phytozome (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov</a>) were aligned to the assembly with TBLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403â410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1573">38</a></sup>, and then GeMoMa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Keilwagen, J. et al. Using intron position conservation for homology-based gene prediction. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e89âe89 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1577">39</a></sup> was used to construct exact gene models from all initially aligned coding sequences. Additionally, RNA-Seq data from five different tissues of <i>S. suchowensis</i>, including the roots, stems, leaves, buds and bark, were mapped to the genome assembly using HISAT and Stringtie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1584">40</a></sup>. TransDecoder (<a href="http://transdecoder.github.io">http://transdecoder.github.io</a>) and GeneMarkS-T<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Tang, S., Lomsadze, A. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Identification of protein coding regions in RNA transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, e78âe78 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1592">41</a></sup> were used to identify transcripts from the mapping results. Finally, all gene models predicted from the above three approaches were integrated by using EvidenceModeler (EVM) v1.1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1597">42</a></sup> to generate consensus gene sets and were filtered with PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Campbell, M. A., Haas, B. J., Hamilton, J. P., Mount, S. M. &amp; Buell, C. R. Comprehensive analysis of alternative splicing in rice and comparative analyses with Arabidopsis. BMC Genomics 7, 327 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1601">43</a></sup> to detect spliced gene structures and generate non-redundant gene models.</p><p>Functional annotations of the predicted genes were further assigned with BLAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinforma. 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1608">44</a></sup> according to the best match of the alignments against the non-redundant plant protein database and non-redundant nucleotide and protein sequences from the NCBI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Marchler-Bauer, A. et al. CDD: a Conserved Domain Database for the functional annotation of proteins. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D225âD229 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1612">45</a></sup>. The Gene Ontology (GO) terms for each gene were obtained from the GO database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Dimmer, E. C. et al. The UniProt-GO annotation database in 2011. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D565âD570 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1616">46</a></sup>. The protein motifs and domains were predicted by using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Mitchell, A. et al. The InterPro protein families database: the classification resource after 15 years. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D213âD221 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1620">47</a></sup> to search against various domain databases. The tRNA genes were searched by using tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1624">48</a></sup> software with the eukaryote parameters. Additionally, microRNA and rRNA genes were identified by using INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Nawrocki, E. P. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics 29, 2933â2935 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1629">49</a></sup> to search the miRBase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Griffiths-Jones, S., Grocock, R. J., Van Dongen, S., Bateman, A. &amp; Enright, A. J. miRBase: microRNA sequences, targets and gene nomenclature. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, D140âD144 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1633">50</a></sup> and Pfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Griffiths-Jones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D121âD124 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1637">51</a></sup> databases, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Assessment of genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>The <i>S. suchowensis</i> BAC sequences were aligned to the updated <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome using the MUMmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="MarÃ§ais, G. et al. MUMmer4: a fast and versatile genome alignment system. PLoS Comput. Biol. 14, e1005944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1656">52</a></sup> program. We mapped DNA sequencing data (&gt;50Ã) from the Illumina HiSeq X ten platform against the first assembly and the updated assembly using BWA-MEM software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, H. Toward better understanding of artifacts in variant calling from high-coverage samples. Bioinformatics 30, 2843â2851 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1660">27</a></sup> and then compared the mapping status of the two genomes with SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1664">28</a></sup>. The transcriptomic short reads derived from different tissues of <i>S. suchowensis</i> (including the roots, stems, leaves, buds, and bark) from the previously published genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Dai, X. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1672">8</a></sup> were aligned to both genome assemblies with STAR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Dobin, A. et al. STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29, 15â21 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1676">53</a></sup>. BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1680">16</a></sup> was used to evaluate the completeness of the assembled genome. Orthofinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1684">54</a></sup> was used to classify the orthologs between <i>S. suchowensis</i> v2.0 and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> v3.0. We used BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinforma. 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1695">44</a></sup> to identify the common genes between <i>S. suchowensis</i> v2.0 and <i>S. suchowensis</i> v1.0. GO enrichment analysis was carried out with the clusterProfiler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Yu, G., Wang, L. G., Han, Y. &amp; He, Q. Y. clusterProfiler: an R package for comparing biological themes among gene clusters. Omics: J. Integr. Biol. 16, 284â287 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1705">55</a></sup> R package.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Gene family analysis</h3><p>We used two software programs, BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinforma. 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1717">44</a></sup> and HMMER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Finn, R. D., Clements, J. &amp; Eddy, S. R. HMMER web server: interactive sequence similarity searching. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, W29âW37 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1721">56</a></sup>, to search for homologous proteins harboring the conserved domains of related gene families in <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome v2.0 with an expected value (<i>e</i>-value) threshold ofâ&lt;1e-10. The predicted protein sequences were further confirmed using both the NCBI conserved domain database (CDD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Marchler-Bauer, A. et al. CDD: a Conserved Domain Database for the functional annotation of proteins. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D225âD229 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1731">45</a></sup> tool and the Simple Modular Architecture Research Tool (SMART)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Letunic, I. &amp; Bork, P. 20 years of the SMART protein domain annotation resource. Nucleic Acids Res. 46, D493âD496 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1736">57</a></sup> to analyze the conserved domains. The final deduced full-length amino acid sequences were aligned by using the ClustalW program with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Larkin, M. A. et al. Clustal W and clustal X version 2.0. Bioinformatics 23, 2947â2948 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1740">58</a></sup>. The phylogenetic tree was constructed using MEGA X via the neighbor-joining (NJ) method with 1000 bootstrap iterations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Li, M., Knyaz, C. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA X: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis across computing platforms. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 1547â1549 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1744">59</a></sup> and visualized using the Interactive Tree of Life (iTOL) web tool (<a href="http://itol.embl.de">http://itol.embl.de</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Letunic, I. &amp; Bork, P. Interactive tree of life (iTOL) v3: an online tool for the display and annotation of phylogenetic and other trees. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, W242âW245 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1752">60</a></sup>.</p><p>We performed a pathway enrichment analysis at the KEGG pathway database (<a href="http://www.genome.jp/kegg">http://www.genome.jp/kegg</a>) to identify enriched metabolic or signal transduction pathways associated with salicylic acid. The classification of biological terms and the analysis of the enrichment of the salicylic acid biosynthesis and metabolism clusters in the KEGG pathways were completed with the clusterProfiler R package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Yu, G., Wang, L. G., Han, Y. &amp; He, Q. Y. clusterProfiler: an R package for comparing biological themes among gene clusters. Omics: J. Integr. Biol. 16, 284â287 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1763">55</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">WGD analysis</h3><p>We identified the collinearity within the assembled <i>S. suchowensis</i> v2.0 chromosomes, as well as that between the <i>S. suchowensis</i> v2.0 and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> v3.0 chromosomes. By running a BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinforma. 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1784">44</a></sup> alignment for each genome pair, we searched for putative paralogous and orthologous genes within and between genomes. Fourfold degenerate sites were located, and the 4DTV values were calculated using in-house Perl scripts. The 4DTV range associated with salicoid duplication was determined by plotting the distribution frequency histogram of 4DTV values. For each salicoid duplicated gene pair, Ka and Ks values and Ka/Ks ratios were calculated using the Nei-Gojobori algorithm implemented in KaKs_Calculator 2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Wang, D., Zhang, Y., Zhang, Z., Zhu, J. &amp; Yu, J. KaKs_Calculator 2.0: a toolkit incorporating gamma-series methods and sliding window strategies. Genomics, Proteom. Bioinforma. 8, 77â80 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1788">61</a></sup>. The boxplot of Ka/Ks values was generated with R software (version 3.6.1; <a href="http://www.r-project.org">www.r-project.org</a>). The synteny blocks were identified and visualized by using the MCSCAN toolkit implemented in Python [<a href="https://github.com/tanghaibao/jcvi/wiki/MCscan">https://github.com/tanghaibao/jcvi/wiki/MCscan</a>-(Python-version)]. The image of the entire <i>S. suchowensis</i> genome was produced using Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639â1645 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0268-6#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d19879e1804">62</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section>
